<script>
	function goToTopic(topic) {
		var topicMap = applySpecialCharacterMapping(topic); 
		var url = "/xpl/topicbrowse.jsp?topic=" + escape(topicMap);
		window.top.location.href = url;
	}
</script>

			<!-- ngIf: ::vm.details.abstract --><div ng-if="::vm.details.abstract" class="ng-scope">
				<strong>Abstract:</strong>
				<div class="abstract-text ng-binding" ng-bind-html="::vm.details.abstract">Passive radio frequency (RF) tags in the UHF and microwave bands have drawn considerable attention because of their great potential for use in many radio frequency identification (RFID) applications. However, more basic research is needed to increase the range and reliability of a passive RF tag's radio link, particularly when the RF tag is placed onto any lossy dielectric or metallic surface. This paper presents two new useful forms of the radio link budget that describe the power link of an RF tag system when the tag is attached to an object. These radio link budgets are dependent upon the gain penalty, a term which quantifies the reduction in RF tag antenna gain due to material attachment. A series of measurements, or radio assay, was used to measure the far-field gain pattern and gain penalty of several flexible 915 MHz antennas when attached to cardboard, pine plywood, acrylic, deionized water, ethylene glycol, ground beef, and an aluminum slab. It is shown that the gain penalty due to material attachment can result in more than 20 dB of excess loss in the backscatter communication link.</div>
			</div><!-- end ngIf: ::vm.details.abstract -->			
			<!-- ngIf: ::vm.details.scope -->
			<!-- ngIf: ::vm.details.purpose -->

<div id="BodyWrapper" class="ArticlePage" xmlns:ieee="http://www.ieeexplore.ieee.org"><div id="article">
<div class="section" id="sec1"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION I.</div><h2>Introduction</h2></div><p>Passive modulated backscatter RF tags are transponders that communicate with an interrogator, or reader, using far-field electromagnetic waves. These tags operate in the UHF and microwave frequency bands and typically modulate the radiation scattered from the tag antenna using load modulation. Though the basic operating principles of backscatter RF tags can be traced to 1948 <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref1" id="context_ref_1_1">[1]</a>, only recently has the use of RF tags for radio frequency identification (RFID) found application in inventory management, access control, toll collection, animal tracking and a host of other applications <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref2" id="context_ref_2_1">[2]</a>. Though RFID tag usage is expected to significantly increase in coming years, the high cost and poor performance of passive RFID tags have limited their implementation thus far. For their widespread use to become reality, much research is needed to lower the tag cost, decrease the tag footprint, and improve the tag performance and reliability.</p><p>RF tag performance is affected by many factors, including the electromagnetic properties of objects near or in contact with the tag antenna. Although little literature has been published on this topic, Foster and Burberry <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref3" id="context_ref_3_1">[3]</a> have performed basic measurements of an antenna near several objects. Raumonen et al. <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref4" id="context_ref_4_1">[4]</a> have studied a similar problem through simulation. Dobkin and Weigand <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref5" id="context_ref_5_1">[5]</a> have also experimentally shown a decrease in the read range of several RF tags near a metal plate and a water filled container along with changes in the RF tag antenna input impedance near metal.</p><p>This paper presents new forms of the power and backscatter communication radio link budgets that allow the RF tag designer to quantify the effects of RF tag material attachment. An important term in these radio link budgets is the <i>gain penalty</i>, or the decrease in RF tag antenna gain from its free space value when attached to a material. Using a <i>radio assay</i>, or series of tests, the gain penalty and gain pattern of several flexible, 915 MHz folded dipole RF tag antennas are measured on materials with varying electromagnetic properties.</p></div>
<div class="section" id="sec2"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION II.</div><h2>Radio Link Budgets</h2></div><p>Equation <a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn1" href="#deqn1" class="fulltext-link">(1)</a> is the power radio link budget that describes the amount of power available to the RF tag for operation. width: 0px; height: 2.165em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.063em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 0.603em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-15">\sigma</script></tex-math></inline-formula></td><td><p>the radar cross section of the RF tag.</p></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="figure figure-full" id="fig1"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/33542/1643635/1643635-fig-1-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/33542/1643635/1643635-fig-1-small.gif" alt="Figure 1" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 1. </b><fig><p>The radio assay experimental setup for measurement of GP, the RF tag antenna gain penalty when attached to a material.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/1643635/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div> All other terms are defined in <a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn1" href="#deqn1" class="fulltext-link">(1)</a> and all antennas are assumed to have an impedance and polarization match. The radar cross section (RCS) of the RF tag is primarily due to scattering by the RF tag antenna and can be written as the sum of a structural mode and an antenna mode <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref6" id="context_ref_6_2">[6]</a>. The scattered fields due to the structural mode are radiated by currents induced by the incident field on the surface of the antenna. The structural mode is not dependent upon the RF tag antenna load and therefore, power scattered due to this term is not modulated. The modulated scattered power, or modulated backscatter, is proportional to the antenna mode of the RCS and can be written (in the linear scale) as <disp-formula id="deqn3" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><div class="MathJax_Display"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-16-Frame" tabindex="0" style="width: 100%;"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-395" role="math" style="display: inline-block; <div class="section" id="sec3"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION III.</div><h2>Methods</h2></div><div class="section_2" id="sec3a"><h3>A. The Radio Assay</h3><p>A radio assay,<a ref-type="fn" anchor="fn1" class="footnote-link"><sup>1.</sup></a> shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig1" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 1</a>, was used to measure the gain of several RF tag antennas attached to materials with varying electromagnetic properties. The gains were calculated using <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-20-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-665" role="math" style="display: inline-block; </p><div class="figure figure-full table" id="table1"><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Table I </b>Measured average gain penalties for 915 MHz half-wave folded dipole placed on objects with varying electrical properties and dimensions<div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></div><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/33542/1643635/1643635-table-1-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/33542/1643635/1643635-table-1-small.gif" alt="Table I" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div></div>
<div class="figure figure-full" id="fig2"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/33542/1643635/1643635-fig-2-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/33542/1643635/1643635-fig-2-small.gif" alt="Figure 2" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 2. </b><fig><p>(a) The feed of the silver RF tag antenna composed of a quarter wave balun, CPS transmission line, and clamp fixture. (b) The PVC antenna mount holding an RF tag antenna attached to pine plywood, the quarter wave balun and the tunable impedance transformation network.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/1643635/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<div class="figure figure-full" id="fig3"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/33542/1643635/1643635-fig-3-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/33542/1643635/1643635-fig-3-small.gif" alt="Figure 3" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 3. </b><fig><p>A planar folded dipole RF tag antenna for use in the radio assay at 915 MHz. It has an estimated free space input impedance of 233 <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-25-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-866" role="math" style="display: inline-block; width: 0.995em;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.797em; height: 0px; font-size: 126%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.26em 1000.73em 2.384em -999.997em); top: -2.179em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-867" style="display: inline-block;"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-868" style="display: inline-block;"><img src="http://staticieeexplore.ieee.org/xploreAssets/MathJax-2.6.1/fonts/HTML-CSS/TeX/png/Main/Regular/336/03A9.png?rev=2.6.1" style="width: 11px; height: 11px; margin-right: 0.069em;" /></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.186em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.079em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.088em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-25">\Omega</script></tex-math></inline-formula>.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/1643635/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<p></p><p>Since the impedance of an antenna varies when placed on a material, a tunable impedance transformation network, shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig1" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 1</a>, was used to achieve a conjugate match between the RF tag antenna and the 50 <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-26-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-869" role="math" style="display: inline-block; width: 0.844em;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.724em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.324em 1000.66em 2.345em -999.997em); top: -2.159em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-870" style="display: inline-block;"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-871" style="display: inline-block;"><img src="http://staticieeexplore.ieee.org/xploreAssets/MathJax-2.6.1/fonts/HTML-CSS/TeX/png/Main/Regular/336/03A9.png?rev=2.6.1" style="width: 11px; height: 11px; margin-right: 0.063em;" /></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.165em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.063em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 0.87em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-26">\Omega</script></tex-math></inline-formula> coaxial feed line <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref7" id="context_ref_7_3a">[7]</a>. All other devices in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig1" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 1</a> were impedance matched and the antennas were oriented for a polarization match. To ensure accurate measurements, the experimental setup was calibrated by removing the the antennas and measuring the losses in the transmitting and receiving portions of the system.</p><p>The quarter wave coaxial balun and coplanar strip (CPS) transmission line used to feed the RF tag antennas are shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig2" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 2(a)</a>, <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref7" id="context_ref_7_3a">[7]</a>. Each RF tag antenna was connected to the CPS line using a clamp to press the antenna against the two conducting strips of the CPS line. Every effort was made to reduce coupling of the antenna to surrounding objects: the clamp fixture was made from unplated FR4 and nylon, the quarter wave coaxial balun was <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-27-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-872" role="math" style="display: inline-block; width: 3.727em;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 3.366em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.324em 1003.37em 2.526em -999.997em); top: -2.159em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-873" style="display: inline-block;"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-874" style="display: inline-block;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-875" style="display: inline-block;"><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-876" style="display: inline-block;"><img src="http://staticieeexplore.ieee.org/xploreAssets/MathJax-2.6.1/fonts/HTML-CSS/TeX/png/Main/Regular/336/2248.png?rev=2.6.1" style="width: 11px; RF Tag Antennas</h3><p>The RF tag antennas tested in the radio assay were half-wave, planar folded dipoles, as shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig3" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 3</a>. Each RF tag antenna was designed for an estimated input impedance of 233 <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-30-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-901" role="math" style="display: inline-block; width: 0.844em;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.724em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.324em 1000.66em 2.345em -999.997em); top: -2.159em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-902" style="display: inline-block;"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-903" style="display: inline-block;"><img src="http://staticieeexplore.ieee.org/xploreAssets/MathJax-2.6.1/fonts/HTML-CSS/TeX/png/Main/Regular/336/03A9.png?rev=2.6.1" style="width: 11px; height: 11px; margin-right: 0.063em;" /></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.165em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.063em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 0.87em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-30">\Omega</script></tex-math></inline-formula> at 915 MHz. Two of the RF tag antennas were made using an electroless silver and an electroless copper plating process on flexible, 25.4 <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-31-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-904" role="math" style="display: inline-block; width: 0.604em;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.544em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.565em 1000.54em 2.526em -999.997em); top: -2.159em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-905" style="display: inline-block;"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-906" style="display: inline-block;"><img src="http://staticieeexplore.ieee.org/xploreAssets/MathJax-2.6.1/fonts/HTML-CSS/TeX/png/Math/Italic/336/03BC.png?rev=2.6.1" style="width: 9px; height: 11px; vertical-align: -4px; margin-right: 0.003em;" /></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.165em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.263em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 0.803em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-31">\mu</script></tex-math></inline-formula> m thick polyethylene terephthalate (PET) substrates. Since these prototype antennas were very thin and lossy, a baseline antenna was also tested for comparison. The baseline antenna was milled on 1 oz. copper-clad FR4 and had negligible ohmic losses (measured at dc).</p></div><div class="section_2" id="sec3c"><h3>C. Radio Assay Materials</h3><p>Seven materials, listed in <a ref-type="table" anchor="table1" class="fulltext-link">Table I</a>, were tested in the radio assay. These materials were chosen because they are commonly used, are likely candidates for RF tag attachment, and provide a variety of complex permittivities and loss tangents. In each test, the block of material to which the RF tag antenna was attached was composed of several layers of the material. The dimensions in <a ref-type="table" anchor="table1" class="fulltext-link">Table I</a> are the approximate dimensions for each material block. The electromagnetic properties of these materials were estimated from the permittivity and loss tangent values of similar materials given by Von Hippel <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref8" id="context_ref_8_3c">[8]</a>. These estimates, given in <a ref-type="table" anchor="table1" class="fulltext-link">Table I</a>, were interpolated to 915 MHz from Von Hippel's data at 300 MHz and 3 GHz and are presented as ârules of thumbâ, recognizing that the actual material properties may vary slightly. The RF tag antennas were attached to each of these materials using thin adhesive tape. For the liquid materials, the antenna was attached to a waxed cardboard carton containing the liquid. Due to the CPS transmission line feed and the clamp fixture, the antennas were not able to lie perfectly flat against each material. However, since the distance that they were separated from the material or bent to touch the material was electromagnetically small <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-36-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-923" role="math" style="display: inline-block; width: 4.808em;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 4.327em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.264em 1004.27em 2.586em -999.997em); top: -2.159em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-924" style="display: inline-block;"><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-925" style="display: inline-block;"><img src="http://staticieeexplore.ieee.org/xploreAssets/MathJax-2.6.1/fonts/HTML-CSS/TeX/png/Main/Regular/336/0028.png?rev=2.6.1" style="width: 5px; height: 16px; vertical-align: -4px; margin-right: 0.063em;" /></span><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-926" style="display: inline-block;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-927" style="display: inline-block;"><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-928" style="display: inline-block;"><img src="http://staticieeexplore.ieee.org/xploreAssets/MathJax-2.6.1/fonts/HTML-CSS/TeX/png/Main/Regular/336/2248.png?rev=2.6.1" style="width: 11px; height: 7px; vertical-align: 1px; margin-right: 0.063em;" /></span></span></span><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-929" style="display: inline-block;"><img src="http://staticieeexplore.ieee.org/xploreAssets/MathJax-2.6.1/fonts/HTML-CSS/TeX/png/Main/Regular/336/0031.png?rev=2.6.1" style="width: 7px; height: 10px; margin-right: 0.063em;" /></span><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-930" style="display: inline-block;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-931" style="display: inline-block;"><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-932" style="display: inline-block;"><img src="http://staticieeexplore.ieee.org/xploreAssets/MathJax-2.6.1/fonts/HTML-CSS/TeX/png/Main/Regular/336/002F.png?rev=2.6.1" style="width: 7px; height: 16px; vertical-align: -4px; margin-right: 0.063em;" /></span></span></span><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-933" style="display: inline-block;"><img src="http://staticieeexplore.ieee.org/xploreAssets/MathJax-2.6.1/fonts/HTML-CSS/TeX/png/Main/Regular/336/0035.png?rev=2.6.1" style="width: 7px; height: 11px; vertical-align: 0px; margin-right: 0.063em;" /><img src="http://staticieeexplore.ieee.org/xploreAssets/MathJax-2.6.1/fonts/HTML-CSS/TeX/png/Main/Regular/336/0030.png?rev=2.6.1" style="width: 7px; height: 11px; vertical-align: 0px; margin-right: 0.063em;" /></span><span class="msubsup" id="MathJax-Span-934" style="display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.964em; height: 0px;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.186em 1000.54em 4.207em -999.997em); top: -4.021em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-935" style="display: inline-block;"><img src="http://staticieeexplore.ieee.org/xploreAssets/MathJax-2.6.1/fonts/HTML-CSS/TeX/png/Math/Italic/336/03BB.png?rev=2.6.1" style="width: 9px; height: 11px; vertical-align: 0px; margin-right: 0.003em;" /></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -3.901em; left: 0.544em;"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-936" style="display: inline-block;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-937" style="display: inline-block;"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-938" style="display: inline-block; font-size: 70.7%;"><img src="http://staticieeexplore.ieee.org/xploreAssets/MathJax-2.6.1/fonts/HTML-CSS/TeX/png/Math/Italic/238/006F.png?rev=2.6.1" style="width: 5px; height: 5px; margin-right: 0.003em;" /></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span></span></span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-939" style="display: inline-block;"><img src="http://staticieeexplore.ieee.org/xploreAssets/MathJax-2.6.1/fonts/HTML-CSS/TeX/png/Main/Regular/336/0029.png?rev=2.6.1" style="width: 5px; height: 16px; vertical-align: -4px; margin-right: 0.063em;" /></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.165em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.33em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.203em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-36">({\approx} 1/50\lambda_{o})</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, this irregularity was neglected.</p></div></div>
<div class="section" id="sec4"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION IV.</div><h2>Radio Assay Results</h2></div><div class="section_2" id="sec4a"><h3>A. Gain Patterns</h3><p>The free-space gain pattern measurements, shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig4" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 4(a)</a>, closely resemble those of an ideal dipole: the patterns contain no spurious lobes or distortion and are free from the effects of multi-path fading. For each gain pattern test, the tunable impedance transformation network achieved a return loss greater than 46 dB and had an insertion loss of less than 2 dB. These parameters were measured to ensure a proper impedance match was reached. The gain patterns measured on the radio assay materials are shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig4" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 4(b)</a>â<a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig4" class="fulltext-link">(f)</a>. The cardboard gain patterns (not shown) are almost identical to the free space patterns. The acrylic and pine plywood gain patterns both display a pattern that is slightly larger in region two than in region one. This pattern enlargement is attributed to an increase in the near field power coupled into the material due to its high permittivity relative to air <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref9" id="context_ref_9_4a">[9]</a>. The gain patterns measured on the aluminum slab<a ref-type="fn" anchor="fn3" class="footnote-link"><sup>3.</sup></a>, ethylene glycol, and ground beef all show a decrease in the nulls and are more omnidirectional. The de-ionized water patterns are unique, with three distinct lobes centered at 0Â° and pattern enlargement in region two. These gain pattern distortions cannot be attributed to a single phenomenon. The surface waves in the material, the diffraction around the material, the permittivity of the material, and the loss tangent of the material all have an effect.</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig4"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/33542/1643635/1643635-fig-4-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/33542/1643635/1643635-fig-4-small.gif" alt="Figure 4" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 4. </b><fig><p>The measured E-plane gain patterns of each half-wave folded dipole in (a) free space, attached to (b) pine plywood, (c) de-ionized water, (d) ethylene glycol, (e) ground beef, and (f) aluminum sheet metal at 915 MHz. Each pattern was normalized to the maximum of the free space baseline gain pattern.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/1643635/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div></div><div class="section_2" id="sec4b"><h3>B. Gain Penalties</h3><p><a ref-type="table" anchor="table1" class="fulltext-link">Table I</a> shows the average RF tag antenna gain penalty, AGP, calculated for each material. The AGP was found by averaging, in the linear scale, the GP values measured for each antenna on the particular material. As <a ref-type="table" anchor="table1" class="fulltext-link">Table I</a> shows, the AGP increases with the increasing loss tangent of each material. The individual GP measurements exhibit high precision for the dielectric materials with a small loss tangent (i.e., cardboard, acrylic, pine plywood, and deionized water), varying at most by 0.9 dB. However, for the highly conductive materials, the GP for each antenna varies by 2.1, 8.4, and 4.2 dB on ethylene glycol, ground beef, and the aluminum slab, respectively. Although measurements indicate that an excellent impedance match was achieved, the impedance matching network may have reached its tuning limit on the highly conductive materials. In this case, the AGP values are pessimistic since mismatch losses would have been included with pattern and efficiency losses due to material attachment. In addition, the AGP values are dependent upon the dimensions of the material. Hence, the AGP values are intended for use as a ârule of thumbâ to allow the RF tag designer to make educated decisions in the design of the RF tag system radio link. The AGP values presented in <a ref-type="table" anchor="table1" class="fulltext-link">Table I</a>, when substituted for GP in <a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn1" href="#deqn1" class="fulltext-link">(1)</a> and <a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn4" href="#deqn4" class="fulltext-link">(4)</a>, show that the effects of material attachment can be significant. In the case of the aluminum slab with an AGP of 10.4 dB, material losses cause 20.8 dB of excess loss in the backscatter communication radio link budget.</p></div></div>
<div class="section" id="sec5"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION V.</div><h2>Summary</h2></div><p>This paper presents two new forms of the radio link budget that allow the RF tag designer to quantify the reduction in RF tag antenna performance due to material attachment. A radio assay was used to measure the AGP and gain patterns of three 915 MHz, folded dipole RF tag antennas attached to seven different materials. The measured gain patterns show significant distortion due to the permittivity and loss tangent of the material, surface waves, and diffraction. The measured AGP increases with the increasing loss tangent of the material from 0.9 dB on cardboard to 10.4 dB on the aluminum slab. It is shown that the gain penalty due to the aluminum slab can cause more than 20 dB of excess loss in the backscatter communication link.</p></div>
<h3>ACKNOWLEDGMENT</h3><p>The authors would like to thank Y. Lu, J. Prothro, and C. Durkin for their assistance in this research.</p></div></div>

<script>

function getCookie(name) {
	var nameEQ = name + "=";
	var ca = document.cookie.split(';');
	for(var i=0;i &lt; ca.length;i++) {
		var c = ca[i];
		while (c.charAt(0)==' ') c = c.substring(1,c.length);
		if (c.indexOf(nameEQ) == 0) return c.substring(nameEQ.length,c.length);
	}
	return null;
}

j$('document').ready(function(){
	if(getCookie('legacyUserName')!=null)
	{
		if(IBP_WS_ENABLED_FLAG){
		Modal.refreshLegacyAccountTransition('/xpl/mwLegacyAccountTransition.jsp');
		}
	}
});

</script>

However, more basic research is needed to increase the range and reliability of a passive RF tag's radio link, particularly when the RF tag is placed onto any lossy dielectric or metallic surface. This paper presents two new useful forms of the radio link budget that describe the power link of an RF tag system when the tag is attached to an object. These radio link budgets are dependent upon the gain penalty, a term which quantifies the reduction in RF tag antenna gain due to material attachment. A series of measurements, or radio assay, was used to measure the far-field gain pattern and gain penalty of several flexible 915 MHz antennas when attached to cardboard, pine plywood, acrylic, deionized water, ethylene glycol, ground beef, and an aluminum slab. It is shown that the gain penalty due to material attachment can result in more than 20 dB of excess loss in the backscatter communication
